{
  "content": "# Terraform Plan/Apply Pipeline Architecture\n\n## Overview\n\nTerraform's plan and apply pipeline is built on a sophisticated architecture that combines directed acyclic graphs (DAGs), plugin-based provider management, concurrent state synchronization, and a extensible hook system. This document describes the internal architecture that enables Terraform to safely plan and execute infrastructure changes in a declarative, concurrent manner.\n\nThe core insight of Terraform's architecture is the separation of concerns:\n- **Planning**: The plan phase reads configuration and current state, then delegates to providers to determine what changes are needed\n- **Applying**: The apply phase executes the changes described by the plan, updating infrastructure and state\n\nBoth phases rely on a common graph-based execution engine that respects resource dependencies, manages provider lifecycle, and provides extension points for tooling.\n\n---\n\n## 1. Graph Builder System\n\n### 1.1 Overview\n\nTerraform constructs an execution graph for each operation (plan, apply, validate, etc.). The graph represents all resources, data sources, modules, variables, outputs, and their dependencies. Rather than hardcoding the graph structure, Terraform uses a **builder pattern** with composable **transformers** that incrementally construct the graph.\n\n### 1.2 GraphBuilder Interface\n\nThe `GraphBuilder` interface is the entry point for graph construction:\n\n```go\ntype GraphBuilder interface {\n    Build(addrs.ModuleInstance) (*Graph, tfdiags.Diagnostics)\n}\n```\n\nTerraform provides two primary implementations:\n\n1. **PlanGraphBuilder**: Builds graphs for planning operations (plan, validate, refresh)\n2. **ApplyGraphBuilder**: Builds graphs for applying plans\n\nBoth builders use the **BasicGraphBuilder**, which applies a series of transforms in sequence:\n\n```go\ntype BasicGraphBuilder struct {\n    Steps []GraphTransformer\n    Name  string\n}\n```\n\n### 1.3 Graph Transformers\n\nGraph transformers implement a simple interface:\n\n```go\ntype GraphTransformer interface {\n    Transform(*Graph) error\n}\n```\n\nEach transformer takes a graph and modifies it by adding, removing, or connecting vertices. The builders compose these transformers to progressively build the complete execution graph.\n\n#### Key Transformers (in typical build order):\n\n1. **ConfigTransformer**: Creates nodes for resources and modules from the configuration\n2. **RootVariableTransformer**: Creates nodes for root module input variables\n3. **ModuleVariableTransformer**: Creates nodes for module input variables\n4. **LocalTransformer**: Creates nodes for local value declarations\n5. **OutputTransformer**: Creates nodes for output values\n6. **DiffTransformer** (apply only): Creates resource instance nodes from the plan\n7. **StateTransformer**: Attaches state information to resource nodes\n8. **ProviderTransformer**: Creates and connects provider configuration nodes\n9. **ReferenceTransformer**: Creates edges for resource references (e.g., `aws_instance.web.id`)\n10. **DestroyEdgeTransformer**: Adds edges for create-before-destroy behavior\n11. **TargetTransformer**: Prunes the graph to only targeted resources (when `-target` is used)\n12. **PruneTransformer**: Removes unreferenced nodes\n\nThe transformer pattern allows:\n- **Modularity**: Each concern (references, state, providers) is isolated\n- **Testability**: Individual transformers can be tested independently\n- **Extensibility**: Custom transformers can be injected for specialized behavior\n\n### 1.4 Graph Structure\n\nThe resulting graph is a directed acyclic graph (DAG) where:\n\n- **Vertices** represent execution units:\n  - `NodeAbstractResource`: A resource declaration in configuration\n  - `NodeAbstractResourceInstance`: A specific instance of a resource (e.g., `aws_instance.web[0]`)\n  - `NodeAbstractProvider`: A provider configuration\n  - `NodeAbstractVariable`: An input variable\n  - `NodeAbstractLocalValue`: A local value\n  - `NodeAbstractOutput`: An output value\n  - Module expansion nodes\n  - Check nodes (for test/check blocks)\n\n- **Edges** represent dependencies:\n  - **Explicit dependencies**: From `depends_on` declarations or references to other resources\n  - **Implicit dependencies**: From attribute interpolations (e.g., `aws_instance.app.security_groups = [aws_security_group.app.id]`)\n  - **Provider dependencies**: From `provider` meta-argument\n  - **Implicit ordering**: From create-before-destroy (CBDs) or other lifecycle features\n\n### 1.5 Resource Instance Expansion\n\nTerraform supports dynamic instance expansion through `count` and `for_each`. The graph builder handles this through:\n\n1. **Instance Expander**: A component (`instances.Expander`) that tracks which resource instances should be created based on variable evaluation\n2. **Partial Expansion**: During planning, expressions may reference unknown values (e.g., from other resources). The expander handles both:\n   - **Known instances**: Concrete instance addresses (e.g., `aws_instance.web[0]`)\n   - **Unknown instances**: Partially expanded addresses representing multiple possible instances\n\n3. **Deferred Resources**: If expansion depends on unknown values, Terraform can defer planning these resources to a later plan phase\n\n### 1.6 Module Graph Construction\n\nModule expansion follows a similar pattern:\n- Module instance expansion is tracked during graph walking\n- Child module graphs are built dynamically as the parent graph is walked\n- Module variables are connected from parent module outputs\n- The `ModuleInstanceExpander` manages the expansion of module instances from `count`/`for_each`\n\n---\n\n## 2. Provider Interface and Lifecycle\n\n### 2.1 Provider Architecture Overview\n\nTerraform's provider system is plugin-based. Providers are:\n- Loaded as external processes (over gRPC)\n- Initialized lazily (only when needed)\n- Shared across multiple resource instances\n- Cached per module instance to avoid redundant initialization\n\n### 2.2 Provider Plugin Communication\n\nProviders communicate with Terraform Core via the **gRPC provider protocol** (currently protocol v6):\n\n```\nTerraform Core\n      \u2193 (gRPC)\nProvider Process\n      \u2193 (method calls)\nProvider Implementation\n```\n\nThe protocol defines:\n- **Capabilities negotiation**: Provider declares supported resource types, data source types, and function names\n- **Schema retrieval**: Terraform requests the schema for resources/data sources\n- **Plan/Apply delegation**: Terraform sends state and configuration, provider returns planned/applied state\n- **Validation**: Schemas and values are validated against provider-defined schemas\n\n### 2.3 Provider Initialization and Caching\n\nProvider initialization follows this flow:\n\n1. **Discovery Phase** (before graph building):\n   - Terraform locates provider binaries\n   - Validates provider checksums and signatures\n   - Caches provider metadata\n\n2. **Graph Walking Phase** (during execution):\n   - When a provider node is visited, `InitProvider()` is called on the `EvalContext`\n   - The provider process is spawned if not already running\n   - The provider's schema is retrieved and cached\n   - The provider is configured with the HCL configuration block\n\n3. **Caching**:\n   - Providers are cached in the `ContextGraphWalker`:\n     - `providerCache`: Map of initialized providers by address\n     - `providerSchemas`: Map of cached schemas\n   - A single provider instance handles all resources of that type in its configuration\n   - Providers are stopped gracefully at the end of the operation\n\n### 2.4 Provider Configuration Resolution\n\nProvider configuration is resolved through a multi-step process:\n\n1. **Configuration Body Merging** (`buildProviderConfig`):\n   - Explicit configuration from HCL blocks\n   - Merged with user input (from `-var` or interactive prompts)\n   - Result is an `hcl.Body` ready for evaluation\n\n2. **Provider Inheritance**:\n   - Child modules can inherit provider configuration from parent modules\n   - Explicit `provider` meta-argument overrides inheritance\n   - Provider aliases enable multiple configurations of the same provider type\n\n3. **Configuration Evaluation**:\n   - The merged configuration body is evaluated in the graph walk\n   - Variables and locals are interpolated\n   - The result is a `cty.Value` (Terraform's type system)\n\n4. **Configuration Application**:\n   - `ConfigureProvider()` is called with the evaluated configuration\n   - The provider stores this for use when operating on resources\n\n### 2.5 Resource Operation Delegation\n\nWhen a resource instance is applied:\n\n1. **Prior State**: Retrieved from the state store\n2. **Proposed New State**: Computed from configuration + known values\n3. **Provider Plan** (Plan phase):\n   - Terraform calls `provider.PlanResourceChange()`\n   - Provider receives: prior state, proposed new state, configuration\n   - Provider returns: planned new state + planned action (create/update/delete/no-op)\n   - Provider can suggest replacement if certain attributes require it\n\n4. **Provider Apply** (Apply phase):\n   - Terraform calls `provider.ApplyResourceChange()`\n   - Provider receives: prior state, planned state\n   - Provider executes the actual infrastructure change\n   - Provider returns: final state after applying\n\n### 2.6 Multi-Instance Parallelization\n\nProvider instances are not thread-safe, but Terraform enables parallelism through:\n- **Resource-level parallelization**: Multiple resources can be applied in parallel\n- **Provider connection pooling**: A single provider instance handles sequential calls\n- **Parallelism limit**: Configurable via `-parallelism` flag (default 10)\n- **Semaphore-based throttling**: A `Semaphore` controls concurrent graph node execution\n\n---\n\n## 3. State Management\n\n### 3.1 State Model\n\nTerraform's state is a JSON file containing:\n\n```json\n{\n  \"version\": 4,\n  \"terraform_version\": \"1.9.0\",\n  \"serial\": 42,\n  \"lineage\": \"abc123...\",\n  \"outputs\": {...},\n  \"resources\": [\n    {\n      \"type\": \"aws_instance\",\n      \"name\": \"web\",\n      \"instances\": [\n        {\n          \"index_key\": 0,\n          \"attributes\": {...},\n          \"private\": {...}\n        }\n      ]\n    }\n  ]\n}\n```\n\nEach resource instance in the state includes:\n- **Attributes**: Values of resource arguments and computed attributes\n- **Metadata**: Provider type, creation timestamp, etc.\n- **Deposed instances**: Previous versions of a resource (for create-before-destroy)\n\n### 3.2 State Synchronization\n\nDuring concurrent graph execution, multiple goroutines access state. Terraform uses **`states.SyncState`** to provide safe concurrent access:\n\n```go\ntype SyncState struct {\n    lock  sync.RWMutex\n    state *State\n}\n\n// Safe operations\nfunc (s *SyncState) Lock() *State { /* acquire write lock */ }\nfunc (s *SyncState) Unlock() { /* release write lock */ }\nfunc (s *SyncState) SyncSnapshotRO() *State { /* get read-only snapshot */ }\n```\n\nKey synchronization strategies:\n\n1. **Snapshot-based Reading**:\n   - Graph nodes read a snapshot of state at the start of evaluation\n   - Multiple nodes can read the same snapshot concurrently\n   - Writes create new snapshots that subsequent nodes see\n\n2. **Write-through Updates**:\n   - When a resource is applied, its state is immediately updated\n   - The update is atomic with respect to concurrent reads\n   - Other graph nodes' snapshots are not invalidated (eventual consistency)\n\n3. **Lock Acquisition**:\n   - Only during write operations (when actually modifying state)\n   - Held for minimal duration (just the state mutation)\n   - Does not block reads (readers use snapshots)\n\n### 3.3 State Snapshots and Isolation\n\nThe `RefreshState` and `PrevRunState` in `ContextGraphWalker` serve specific purposes:\n\n- **State**: The current state being modified by the apply (or plan) operation\n- **RefreshState**: A snapshot of state before refresh (during planning)\n- **PrevRunState**: The state file from the previous Terraform run (for change detection)\n\nThis enables:\n- **Deferred updates**: A resource can update state without affecting other in-flight evaluations\n- **Rollback simulation**: If a node fails, other nodes can still read a consistent state snapshot\n- **Plan preservation**: During planning, original state is preserved separately from computed changes\n\n### 3.4 State Persistence\n\nState is persisted to storage backends through the **backend** interface:\n\n```go\ntype Backend interface {\n    StateMgr(workspace string) (statemgr.Full, error)\n    DeleteWorkspace(name string) error\n    Workspaces() ([]string, error)\n    CurrentWorkspace() string\n}\n```\n\nBackends include:\n- **Local**: File-based state in `.terraform/` directory\n- **Remote** (Terraform Cloud): HTTPS API with versioning and locking\n- **S3, Azure Storage, Consul, etc.**: External storage with state locking\n\n### 3.5 State Locking\n\nFor remote backends, Terraform implements distributed locking:\n\n1. **Lock Acquisition** (before plan/apply):\n   - Terraform requests a lock from the backend\n   - If the lock is held, it waits with exponential backoff\n   - Lock includes machine/user/process information for debugging\n\n2. **Lock Holder Check**:\n   - If lock has stale holder info, Terraform can force-unlock\n   - Force-unlock is a manual operation to prevent accidental state corruption\n\n3. **Lock Release** (after operation):\n   - Lock is released even if errors occur (via defer)\n   - If release fails, a warning is logged but execution continues\n\n### 3.6 State Change Tracking\n\nThe `plans.Changes` object tracks all proposed changes during planning:\n\n```go\ntype Changes struct {\n    Resources []*ResourceInstanceChange\n    Outputs   []*OutputChange\n}\n\ntype ResourceInstanceChange struct {\n    Addr      addrs.AbsResourceInstance\n    DeposedKey DeposedKey\n    Change    Change\n    Provider  addrs.Provider\n    // ...\n}\n```\n\nThis structure:\n- Is computed during the plan phase\n- Is compared against configuration during apply to detect drift\n- Is serialized to the plan file for human review\n- Forms the basis for the apply phase operations\n\n---\n\n## 4. Execution Flow and Hook System\n\n### 4.1 Overall Operation Flow\n\n#### Plan Operation\n\n```\nterraform plan\n    \u2193\nContext.Plan() [context_plan.go]\n    \u2193\n1. Build PlanGraphBuilder\n    \u251c\u2500 ConfigTransformer: Add resources, variables, outputs from config\n    \u251c\u2500 StateTransformer: Attach current state to resource nodes\n    \u251c\u2500 ProviderTransformer: Create provider nodes and edges\n    \u251c\u2500 ReferenceTransformer: Add edges for implicit dependencies\n    \u2514\u2500 ... (more transformers)\n    \u2193\n2. Walk graph with walkPlan operation\n    \u251c\u2500 Visit nodes concurrently (respecting DAG edges)\n    \u251c\u2500 For each resource: call provider.PlanResourceChange()\n    \u251c\u2500 Track proposed changes in plans.Changes\n    \u2514\u2500 Evaluate outputs and locals\n    \u2193\n3. Return Plan object\n    \u251c\u2500 Serialized to .tfplan file\n    \u2514\u2500 Contains: changes, prior state, configuration\n```\n\n#### Apply Operation\n\n```\nterraform apply\n    \u2193\nContext.Apply(plan) [context_apply.go]\n    \u2193\n1. Validate plan (check Applyable, check for errors)\n    \u2193\n2. Build ApplyGraphBuilder\n    \u251c\u2500 ConfigTransformer: Add resources from config\n    \u251c\u2500 DiffTransformer: Create instance nodes from plan\n    \u251c\u2500 StateTransformer: Attach current state to resource nodes\n    \u251c\u2500 ProviderTransformer: Create provider nodes\n    \u2514\u2500 ... (more transformers)\n    \u2193\n3. Walk graph with walkApply operation\n    \u251c\u2500 Visit nodes concurrently (respecting DAG edges)\n    \u251c\u2500 For each resource: call provider.ApplyResourceChange()\n    \u251c\u2500 Update state after each successful apply\n    \u251c\u2500 Call hooks at key points (PreApply, PostApply, etc.)\n    \u2514\u2500 Handle errors: mark as partially applied\n    \u2193\n4. Return updated State object\n    \u251c\u2500 Persisted to state backend\n    \u2514\u2500 Available for next plan\n```\n\n### 4.2 Graph Walking with Concurrency\n\nThe `Graph.Walk()` method executes the graph using the visitor pattern:\n\n```go\nfunc (g *Graph) Walk(walker GraphWalker) tfdiags.Diagnostics {\n    ctx := walker.EvalContext()\n\n    walkFn := func(v dag.Vertex) tfdiags.Diagnostics {\n        // Execute the node\n        if executable, ok := v.(GraphNodeExecutable); ok {\n            return walker.Execute(ctx, executable)\n        }\n        return nil\n    }\n\n    // Walk DAG in dependency order, with concurrency\n    return g.AcyclicGraph.Walk(walkFn)\n}\n```\n\nKey aspects:\n\n1. **Topological Ordering**:\n   - The DAG library ensures nodes are visited only after their dependencies\n   - Dependencies are represented as graph edges\n\n2. **Concurrency**:\n   - Multiple nodes can be visited in parallel if they have no dependencies\n   - Parallelism is controlled by a semaphore (default 10 concurrent operations)\n\n3. **Error Handling**:\n   - If a node returns errors, it's marked as failed\n   - Dependent nodes are still visited (to gather all errors)\n   - The final result includes all collected diagnostics\n\n### 4.3 The Walker Pattern\n\nThe `GraphWalker` interface defines how nodes are executed:\n\n```go\ntype GraphWalker interface {\n    EvalContext() EvalContext\n    enterScope(evalContextScope) EvalContext\n    exitScope(evalContextScope)\n    Execute(EvalContext, GraphNodeExecutable) tfdiags.Diagnostics\n}\n```\n\nThe primary implementation is `ContextGraphWalker`, which:\n\n1. **Manages EvalContext**:\n   - Per-scope `BuiltinEvalContext` instances\n   - Scope = module instance + phase\n   - Enables isolated evaluation of different module instances\n\n2. **Caches Plugins**:\n   - Provider instances and schemas\n   - Provisioner instances and schemas\n   - Reused across multiple node executions\n\n3. **Tracks State**:\n   - Holds reference to `SyncState`\n   - Enables thread-safe state updates\n\n4. **Executes Nodes**:\n   - Calls `node.Execute(ctx, operation)`\n   - Returns diagnostics (errors, warnings)\n\n### 4.4 Node Execution\n\nEach graph node implements the `GraphNodeExecutable` interface:\n\n```go\ntype GraphNodeExecutable interface {\n    Execute(EvalContext, walkOperation) tfdiags.Diagnostics\n}\n```\n\nCommon node types and their execution:\n\n1. **NodeApplyableResourceInstance** (apply phase):\n   ```\n   - Check that planned action matches expected\n   - Get prior state from state store\n   - Call provider.ApplyResourceChange()\n   - Update state with result\n   - Call hooks (PreApply, PostApply)\n   - Run provisioners if configured\n   ```\n\n2. **NodePlanableResourceInstance** (plan phase):\n   ```\n   - Get prior state from state store\n   - Evaluate configuration with known values\n   - Call provider.PlanResourceChange()\n   - Store proposed change in Changes\n   - Call hooks (PreDiff, PostDiff)\n   ```\n\n3. **NodeApplyableProvider** (apply phase):\n   ```\n   - Get or initialize provider\n   - Configure provider with evaluated config\n   - Close provider connection at end\n   ```\n\n4. **NodeApplyableOutput** (apply phase):\n   ```\n   - Evaluate output expression\n   - Update state with new value\n   ```\n\n### 4.5 Hook System\n\nHooks provide extension points for monitoring and controlling execution. The `Hook` interface includes:\n\n```go\ntype Hook interface {\n    // Resource lifecycle hooks\n    PreApply(id HookResourceIdentity, ...) (HookAction, error)\n    PostApply(id HookResourceIdentity, ...) (HookAction, error)\n    PreDiff(id HookResourceIdentity, ...) (HookAction, error)\n    PostDiff(id HookResourceIdentity, ...) (HookAction, error)\n\n    // Provisioning hooks\n    PreProvisionInstance(id HookResourceIdentity, ...) (HookAction, error)\n    PostProvisionInstance(id HookResourceIdentity, ...) (HookAction, error)\n    PreProvisionInstanceStep(id HookResourceIdentity, ...) (HookAction, error)\n    PostProvisionInstanceStep(id HookResourceIdentity, ...) (HookAction, error)\n    ProvisionOutput(id HookResourceIdentity, ...)\n\n    // Refresh hooks\n    PreRefresh(id HookResourceIdentity, ...) (HookAction, error)\n    PostRefresh(id HookResourceIdentity, ...) (HookAction, error)\n\n    // Import hooks\n    PreImportState(id HookResourceIdentity, ...) (HookAction, error)\n    PostImportState(id HookResourceIdentity, ...) (HookAction, error)\n\n    // State hooks\n    PostStateUpdate(new *states.State) (HookAction, error)\n\n    // Lifecycle control\n    Stopping()\n}\n```\n\n#### Hook Execution Flow\n\nHooks are called by nodes during execution:\n\n```go\nfunc (n *NodeApplyableResourceInstance) Execute(ctx EvalContext, ...) tfdiags.Diagnostics {\n    // Before applying, call PreApply hooks\n    action, err := ctx.Hook(func(h Hook) (HookAction, error) {\n        return h.PreApply(resourceID, dk, action, priorState, plannedState)\n    })\n\n    if action == HookActionHalt {\n        return diags.Append(/* halted error */)\n    }\n\n    // Apply the resource\n    newState, err := provider.ApplyResourceChange(...)\n\n    // After applying, call PostApply hooks\n    ctx.Hook(func(h Hook) (HookAction, error) {\n        return h.PostApply(resourceID, dk, newState, err)\n    })\n}\n```\n\n#### Hook Actions\n\nHooks return a `HookAction`:\n- `HookActionContinue`: Continue execution normally\n- `HookActionHalt`: Stop execution of the current operation (e.g., halt plan)\n\n#### Extension Points\n\nHooks enable:\n- **Monitoring**: Log state changes, resource creation/destruction\n- **UI Integration**: Display progress, resource changes\n- **Validation**: Check changes against policies before apply\n- **Integration**: Notify external systems during apply\n- **Debugging**: Record detailed execution traces\n\n### 4.6 Dynamic Expansion of count/for_each\n\nResources with `count` or `for_each` are dynamically expanded during graph walking:\n\n1. **Count/For_each Evaluation**:\n   - The count/for_each expression is evaluated to produce a list/map\n   - If the result is unknown (depends on another resource), expansion is partial/deferred\n\n2. **Instance Registration**:\n   - For each item in the count/for_each, an instance is registered with `instances.Expander`\n   - This creates addresses like `aws_instance.web[0]`, `aws_instance.web[1]`, etc.\n\n3. **Graph Expansion**:\n   - Transformer nodes (`NodeResourceExpand`) add instance nodes to the graph\n   - These instance nodes have dependencies on the resource's count/for_each expression\n   - Dependent resources' reference expressions are expanded (e.g., `aws_instance.web[*].id`)\n\n4. **Deferred Expansion**:\n   - If count/for_each is unknown, the instance expansion node marks it as deferred\n   - The resource is marked as deferred in the plan\n   - On the next plan, it will be planned once the dependency is available\n\nExample:\n\n```hcl\n# Configuration\nresource \"aws_instance\" \"web\" {\n  count = var.num_instances\n  # ...\n}\n\nresource \"aws_lb_target_group_attachment\" \"web\" {\n  count            = var.num_instances\n  target_id        = aws_instance.web[count.index].id\n  target_group_arn = aws_lb_target_group.app.arn\n}\n```\n\nGraph structure:\n```\n- NodeVariableExpand(var.num_instances)\n  \u2193\n- NodeResourceExpand(aws_instance.web)\n  \u251c\u2500 depends on NodeVariableExpand(var.num_instances)\n  \u251c\u2500 creates instance nodes: [0], [1], ...\n  \u2193\n- NodeApplyableResourceInstance(aws_instance.web[0])\n  \u251c\u2500 depends on NodeApplyableProvider(aws)\n  \u251c\u2500 depends on NodeResourceExpand(aws_instance.web)\n  \u2193\n- NodeApplyableResourceInstance(aws_instance.web[1])\n  \u251c\u2500 depends on NodeApplyableProvider(aws)\n  \u251c\u2500 depends on NodeResourceExpand(aws_instance.web)\n  \u2193\n- NodeResourceExpand(aws_lb_target_group_attachment.web)\n  \u251c\u2500 depends on NodeResourceExpand(aws_instance.web)  [for reference expansion]\n  \u251c\u2500 creates instance nodes based on count\n  \u2193\n- NodeApplyableResourceInstance(aws_lb_target_group_attachment.web[0])\n  \u251c\u2500 depends on NodeApplyableResourceInstance(aws_instance.web[0]) [from reference]\n  \u2514\u2500 depends on NodeApplyableResourceInstance(aws_lb_target_group.app)\n```\n\n---\n\n## 5. Extension Points and Customization\n\n### 5.1 Custom Graph Transformers\n\nCallers can inject custom transformers by:\n1. Creating a struct implementing `GraphTransformer`\n2. Adding it to the builder's `Steps` slice at the appropriate position\n\nExample use case: Enforce tagging policy by modifying resource configurations before planning.\n\n### 5.2 Custom Hooks\n\nCallers can register hooks by:\n1. Implementing the `Hook` interface\n2. Passing hooks via `ContextOpts.Hooks` to `NewContext()`\n\nExample use case: Send notifications when a resource is destroyed.\n\n### 5.3 External Provider Configurations\n\nThe `ExternalProviderConfigs` parameter allows:\n1. Pre-configuring provider instances outside the normal flow\n2. Passing these instances into the graph builder\n3. Avoiding redundant provider configuration\n\nExample use case: Using pre-authenticated providers in tests or automation.\n\n### 5.4 Evaluation Overrides\n\nThe testing framework provides `mocking.Overrides` to:\n1. Override resource outputs for testing\n2. Override module outputs for testing\n3. Test conditional logic without actual provider operations\n\n---\n\n## 6. Error Handling and Recovery\n\n### 6.1 Diagnostic Collection\n\nTerraform uses a `tfdiags.Diagnostics` type to collect errors and warnings:\n- **Errors**: Blocks execution and prevents apply\n- **Warnings**: Logged but don't block execution\n- **Source information**: Each diagnostic includes filename and line number\n\n### 6.2 Partial Apply Recovery\n\nIf a resource fails to apply:\n1. The error is recorded in diagnostics\n2. Other resources continue to execute (DAG-dependent resources may fail due to missing state)\n3. The state is partially updated (some resources applied, some not)\n4. On the next apply, Terraform detects the partial state and attempts to converge\n\n### 6.3 Graceful Shutdown\n\nThe `Context.Stop()` method enables graceful shutdown:\n1. Sets `runContextCancel`, triggering `context.Done()` on all goroutines\n2. Providers are notified via `provider.Stop()` to stop long-running operations\n3. The graph walk completes (nodes respect the stop context)\n4. Hooks are notified via `Stopping()` to persist transient state\n\n---\n\n## 7. Performance Characteristics\n\n### 7.1 Parallelization\n\n- **Resource-level**: Multiple independent resources execute in parallel\n- **Parallelism limit**: Configurable via `-parallelism` flag (default 10)\n- **Provider constraint**: A single provider handles sequential calls, but multiple providers can work in parallel\n\n### 7.2 State Snapshot Isolation\n\n- **Read-only snapshots**: Multiple nodes can read state concurrently\n- **Write batching**: State updates are atomic, don't block reads\n- **Minimal locking**: Locks held only during state mutation, not during provider operations\n\n### 7.3 Lazy Plugin Loading\n\n- **On-demand initialization**: Providers are initialized only when needed\n- **Connection pooling**: A single provider connection handles all resources of that type\n- **Early shutdown**: Unused providers are closed before walk completes\n\n### 7.4 Caching\n\n- **Schema caching**: Provider schemas are cached after first retrieval\n- **Configuration caching**: Provider configurations are cached per scope\n- **Instance snapshots**: State snapshots enable efficient concurrent reads\n\n---\n\n## 8. Summary: Data Flow During Plan/Apply\n\n### Plan Phase\n\n```\nConfig Files\n    \u2193\nConfig Parser \u2192 configs.Config\n    \u2193\nState Backend \u2192 states.State\n    \u2193\nPlanGraphBuilder\n    \u251c\u2500 1. ConfigTransformer: Add resource/variable/output nodes\n    \u251c\u2500 2. StateTransformer: Attach current state\n    \u251c\u2500 3. ProviderTransformer: Add provider nodes\n    \u251c\u2500 4. ReferenceTransformer: Add dependency edges\n    \u251c\u2500 5. ... (additional transformers)\n    \u2514\u2500 \u2192 Graph (DAG of execution nodes)\n    \u2193\nGraph.Walk(ContextGraphWalker)\n    \u251c\u2500 For each resource instance:\n    \u2502   \u251c\u2500 Get provider (or initialize it)\n    \u2502   \u251c\u2500 Evaluate configuration\n    \u2502   \u251c\u2500 Call provider.PlanResourceChange()\n    \u2502   \u251c\u2500 Store change in plans.Changes\n    \u2502   \u2514\u2500 Call hooks\n    \u251c\u2500 For each output:\n    \u2502   \u251c\u2500 Evaluate expression\n    \u2502   \u2514\u2500 Store in NamedValues\n    \u2514\u2500 Errors collected in diagnostics\n    \u2193\nplans.Plan (serialized to .tfplan file)\n```\n\n### Apply Phase\n\n```\nplans.Plan (read from .tfplan file)\n    \u2193\nconfigs.Config (re-parsed from disk)\n    \u2193\nstates.State (read from backend)\n    \u2193\nApplyGraphBuilder\n    \u251c\u2500 1. ConfigTransformer: Add resource nodes\n    \u251c\u2500 2. DiffTransformer: Create instance nodes from plan\n    \u251c\u2500 3. StateTransformer: Attach current state\n    \u251c\u2500 4. ProviderTransformer: Add provider nodes\n    \u251c\u2500 5. ... (additional transformers)\n    \u2514\u2500 \u2192 Graph (DAG of execution nodes)\n    \u2193\nGraph.Walk(ContextGraphWalker)\n    \u251c\u2500 For each resource instance in plan:\n    \u2502   \u251c\u2500 Pre-apply hooks\n    \u2502   \u251c\u2500 Get provider (or initialize it)\n    \u2502   \u251c\u2500 Call provider.ApplyResourceChange()\n    \u2502   \u251c\u2500 Update state\n    \u2502   \u251c\u2500 Run provisioners (if configured)\n    \u2502   \u251c\u2500 Post-apply hooks\n    \u2502   \u2514\u2500 Errors mark resource as failed\n    \u251c\u2500 For each output:\n    \u2502   \u251c\u2500 Evaluate expression\n    \u2502   \u2514\u2500 Update state\n    \u2514\u2500 Errors collected in diagnostics\n    \u2193\nState Backend \u2190 states.State (persisted)\n    \u2193\nSuccess/Failure Report\n```\n\n---\n\n## 9. Key Design Principles\n\n1. **Declarative Graph Model**: Infrastructure operations are represented as a DAG, enabling parallel execution and clear dependency management.\n\n2. **Plugin Architecture**: Providers are external processes communicating over gRPC, enabling extensibility without modifying core Terraform.\n\n3. **Separation of Plan and Apply**: Planning is deterministic and doesn't modify state; applying executes the plan and updates state. This enables review and approval between phases.\n\n4. **Concurrent State Safety**: Snapshot-based reading and atomic writes enable safe concurrent access to state without widespread locking.\n\n5. **Hook-based Extensibility**: Numerous hook points allow external tools to integrate with Terraform's execution without modifying core code.\n\n6. **Immutable Snapshots**: During execution, state snapshots remain consistent within a graph node's evaluation, preventing race conditions.\n\n7. **Graceful Degradation**: Partial failures are handled gracefully; subsequent applies can recover from incomplete prior applies.\n\n---\n\n## Conclusion\n\nTerraform's plan/apply architecture combines a sophisticated graph-based execution engine with careful state management, provider isolation, and extensibility through hooks. The architecture enables:\n\n- **Safe Infrastructure Changes**: Plan-then-apply workflow with review capability\n- **Concurrent Operations**: DAG-based parallelization respecting dependencies\n- **Extensibility**: Custom transformers, hooks, and provider implementations\n- **Reliability**: Graceful error handling and partial apply recovery\n- **Performance**: Efficient state snapshots, provider caching, and lazy initialization\n\nThe modular design, with clear boundaries between components (graph building, state management, provider communication, execution), enables both the complexity needed for real-world infrastructure management and the maintainability required for a large, widely-used project.\n",

The builders compose these transformers to progressively build the complete execution graph.\n\n#### Key Transformers (in typical build order):\n\n1. **ConfigTransformer**: Creates nodes for resources and modules from the configuration\n2. **RootVariableTransformer**: Creates nodes for root module input variables\n3. **ModuleVariableTransformer**: Creates nodes for module input variables\n4. **LocalTransformer**: Creates nodes for local value declarations\n5. **OutputTransformer**: Creates nodes for output values\n6. **DiffTransformer** (apply only): Creates resource instance nodes from the plan\n7. **StateTransformer**: Attaches state information to resource nodes\n8. **ProviderTransformer**: Creates and connects provider configuration nodes\n9. **ReferenceTransformer**: Creates edges for resource references (e.g., `aws_instance.web.id`)\n10. **DestroyEdgeTransformer**: Adds edges for create-before-destroy behavior\n11. **TargetTransformer**: Prunes the graph to only targeted resources (when `-target` is used)\n12. **PruneTransformer**: Removes unreferenced nodes\n\nThe transformer pattern allows:\n- **Modularity**: Each concern (references, state, providers) is isolated\n- **Testability**: Individual transformers can be tested independently\n- **Extensibility**: Custom transformers can be injected for specialized behavior\n\n### 1.4 Graph Structure\n\nThe resulting graph is a directed acyclic graph (DAG) where:\n\n- **Vertices** represent execution units:\n  - `NodeAbstractResource`: A resource declaration in configuration\n  - `NodeAbstractResourceInstance`: A specific instance of a resource (e.g., `aws_instance.web[0]`)\n  - `NodeAbstractProvider`: A provider configuration\n  - `NodeAbstractVariable`: An input variable\n  - `NodeAbstractLocalValue`: A local value\n  - `NodeAbstractOutput`: An output value\n  - Module expansion nodes\n  - Check nodes (for test/check blocks)\n\n- **Edges** represent dependencies:\n  - **Explicit dependencies**: From `depends_on` declarations or references to other resources\n  - **Implicit dependencies**: From attribute interpolations (e.g., `aws_instance.app.security_groups = [aws_security_group.app.id]`)\n  - **Provider dependencies**: From `provider` meta-argument\n  - **Implicit ordering**: From create-before-destroy (CBDs) or other lifecycle features\n\n### 1.5 Resource Instance Expansion\n\nTerraform supports dynamic instance expansion through `count` and `for_each`. # Terraform Plan/Apply Pipeline Architecture

## Overview

Terraform's plan and apply pipeline is built on a sophisticated architecture that combines directed acyclic graphs (DAGs), plugin-based provider management, concurrent state synchronization, and a extensible hook system. This document describes the internal architecture that enables Terraform to safely plan and execute infrastructure changes in a declarative, concurrent manner.

The core insight of Terraform's architecture is the separation of concerns:
- **Planning**: The plan phase reads configuration and current state, then delegates to providers to determine what changes are needed
- **Applying**: The apply phase executes the changes described by the plan, updating infrastructure and state

Both phases rely on a common graph-based execution engine that respects resource dependencies, manages provider lifecycle, and provides extension points for tooling.

---

## 1. Graph Builder System

### 1.1 Overview

Terraform constructs an execution graph for each operation (plan, apply, validate, etc.). The graph represents all resources, data sources, modules, variables, outputs, and their dependencies. Rather than hardcoding the graph structure, Terraform uses a **builder pattern** with composable **transformers** that incrementally construct the graph.

### 1.2 GraphBuilder Interface

The `GraphBuilder` interface is the entry point for graph construction:

```go
type GraphBuilder interface {
    Build(addrs.ModuleInstance) (*Graph, tfdiags.Diagnostics)
}
```

Terraform provides two primary implementations:

1. **PlanGraphBuilder**: Builds graphs for planning operations (plan, validate, refresh)
2. **ApplyGraphBuilder**: Builds graphs for applying plans

Both builders use the **BasicGraphBuilder**, which applies a se
